   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Transit of Venus
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>

        Transit of Venus (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .
      </div>

          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Continuous visible spectrum
         </div>
        </div>
        <div style="clear:left">
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">

         </a>

         <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">

      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SDO%27s_Ultra-high_Definition_View_of_2012_Venus_Transit_(304_Angstrom_Full_Disc_02).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4096" data-file-width="4096" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/SDO%27s_Ultra-high_Definition_View_of_2012_Venus_Transit_%28304_Angstrom_Full_Disc_02%29.jpg/220px-SDO%27s_Ultra-high_Definition_View_of_2012_Venus_Transit_%28304_Angstrom_Full_Disc_02%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/SDO%27s_Ultra-high_Definition_View_of_2012_Venus_Transit_%28304_Angstrom_Full_Disc_02%29.jpg/330px-SDO%27s_Ultra-high_Definition_View_of_2012_Venus_Transit_%28304_Angstrom_Full_Disc_02%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/SDO%27s_Ultra-high_Definition_View_of_2012_Venus_Transit_%28304_Angstrom_Full_Disc_02%29.jpg/440px-SDO%27s_Ultra-high_Definition_View_of_2012_Venus_Transit_%28304_Angstrom_Full_Disc_02%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SDO%27s_Ultra-high_Definition_View_of_2012_Venus_Transit_(304_Angstrom_Full_Disc_02).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Image of the
         <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_2012" title="Transit of Venus, 2012">
          2012 transit
         </a>
         taken by NASA's
         <a href="/wiki/Solar_Dynamics_Observatory" title="Solar Dynamics Observatory">
          Solar Dynamics Observatory
         </a>
         spacecraft
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A
       <b>
        transit of Venus
       </b>
       across the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       takes place when the
       <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">
        planet
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
        Venus
       </a>
       passes directly between the Sun and a
       <a href="/wiki/Inferior_and_superior_planets" title="Inferior and superior planets">
        superior planet
       </a>
       , becoming visible against (and hence obscuring a small portion of) the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_disk" title="Solar disk">
        solar disk
       </a>
       . During a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_transit" title="Astronomical transit">
        transit
       </a>
       , Venus can be seen from Earth as a small black disk moving across the face of the Sun. The duration of such transits is usually several hours (the transit of 2012 lasted 6 hours and 40 minutes). A transit
       <a href="/wiki/Occultation#Occultations,_transits_and_eclipses" title="Occultation">
        is similar to
       </a>
       a
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">
        solar eclipse
       </a>
       by the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       . While the diameter of Venus is more than three times that of the Moon, Venus appears smaller, and travels more slowly across the face of the Sun, because it is much farther away from Earth.
      </p>
      <p>
       Transits of Venus are among the rarest of predictable astronomical phenomena.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EarthSky_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-EarthSky-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They occur in a pattern that generally repeats every 243 years, with pairs of transits eight years apart separated by long gaps of 121.5 years and 105.5 years. The periodicity is a reflection of the fact that the
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">
        orbital periods
       </a>
       of Earth and Venus
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance#Coincidental_'near'_ratios_of_mean_motion" title="Orbital resonance">
        are close
       </a>
       to 8:13 and 243:395
       <a href="/wiki/Commensurability_(astronomy)" title="Commensurability (astronomy)">
        commensurabilities
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langford_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Langford-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shortt_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Shortt-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The last transit of Venus was
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_2012" title="Transit of Venus, 2012">
        on 5 and 6 June 2012
       </a>
       , and was the last Venus transit of the 21st century; the prior transit took place
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_2004" title="Transit of Venus, 2004">
        on 8 June 2004
       </a>
       . The previous pair of transits were in December 1874 and December 1882. The next transits of Venus will take place on 10–11 December 2117, and 8 December 2125.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-westfall_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-westfall-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Venus transits are historically of great scientific importance as they were used to gain the first realistic estimates of the size of the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       . Observations of the
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_1639" title="Transit of Venus, 1639">
        1639 transit
       </a>
       , combined with the principle of
       <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">
        parallax
       </a>
       , provided an estimate of the distance between the Sun and the Earth that was more accurate than any other up to that time. The 2012 transit provided scientists with a number of other research opportunities, particularly in the refinement of techniques to be used in the search for
       <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">
        exoplanets
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Conjunctions">
        Conjunctions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Transit_of_Venus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Conjunctions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Transit_diagram_angles.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="295" data-file-width="441" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Transit_diagram_angles.png/220px-Transit_diagram_angles.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Transit_diagram_angles.png/330px-Transit_diagram_angles.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Transit_diagram_angles.png/440px-Transit_diagram_angles.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Transit_diagram_angles.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Diagram of transits of Venus and the angle between the orbital planes of Venus and Earth
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Venus, with an orbit inclined by 3.4° relative to the Earth's, usually appears to pass under (or over) the Sun at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inferior_conjunction" title="Inferior conjunction">
        inferior conjunction
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ESA-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A transit occurs when Venus reaches conjunction with the Sun at or near one of its nodes—the longitude where Venus passes through the Earth's orbital plane (the
       <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">
        ecliptic
       </a>
       )—and appears to pass directly across the Sun. Although the inclination between these two orbital planes is only 3.4°, Venus can be as far as 9.6° from the Sun when viewed from the Earth at inferior conjunction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DE_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DE-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since the
       <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">
        angular diameter
       </a>
       of the Sun is about half a degree, Venus may appear to pass above or below the Sun by more than 18 solar diameters during an ordinary conjunction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ESA-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Sequences of transits usually repeat every 243 years. After this period of time Venus and Earth have returned to very nearly the same point in their respective orbits. During the Earth's 243
       <a href="/wiki/Sidereal_year" title="Sidereal year">
        sidereal orbital periods
       </a>
       , which total 88,757.3 days, Venus completes 395 sidereal orbital periods of 224.701 days each, equal to 88,756.9 Earth days. This period of time corresponds to 152
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synodic_period" title="Synodic period">
        synodic periods
       </a>
       of Venus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FENASA_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FENASA-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The pattern of 105.5, 8, 121.5 and 8 years is not the only pattern that is possible within the 243-year cycle, because of the slight mismatch between the times when the Earth and Venus arrive at the point of conjunction. Prior to 1518, the pattern of transits was 8, 113.5 and 121.5 years, and the eight inter-transit gaps before the AD 546 transit were 121.5 years apart. The current pattern will continue until 2846, when it will be replaced by a pattern of 105.5, 129.5 and 8 years. Thus, the 243-year cycle is relatively stable, but the number of transits and their timing within the cycle will vary over time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FENASA_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FENASA-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FL_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FL-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since the 243:395 Earth:Venus commensurability is only approximate, there are different sequences of transits occurring 243 years apart, each extending for several thousand years, which are eventually replaced by other sequences. For instance, there is a series which ended in 541 BC, and the series which includes 2117 only started in AD 1631.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FENASA_9-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FENASA-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_observation">
        History of observation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Transit_of_Venus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History of observation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_Tablet_of_Ammisaduqa.jpg">
         <img alt="Cuneiform clay tablet of observations" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2743" data-file-width="1627" height="371" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Venus_Tablet_of_Ammisaduqa.jpg/220px-Venus_Tablet_of_Ammisaduqa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Venus_Tablet_of_Ammisaduqa.jpg/330px-Venus_Tablet_of_Ammisaduqa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Venus_Tablet_of_Ammisaduqa.jpg/440px-Venus_Tablet_of_Ammisaduqa.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_Tablet_of_Ammisaduqa.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         "
         <a href="/wiki/Venus_tablet_of_Ammisaduqa" title="Venus tablet of Ammisaduqa">
          Venus Tablet of Ammisaduqa
         </a>
         ", a
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">
          cuneiform
         </a>
         clay tablet of astrological forecasts from the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian" title="Neo-Assyrian">
          Neo-Assyrian
         </a>
         period
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_and_medieval_history">
        Ancient and medieval history
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Transit_of_Venus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient and medieval history">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Ancient
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Indian" title="Ancient Indian">
        Indian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
        Greek
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">
        Egyptian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">
        Babylonian
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">
        Chinese
       </a>
       observers knew of Venus and recorded the planet's motions. The early Greek astronomers called Venus by two names—
       <i>
        Hesperus
       </i>
       the evening star and
       <i>
        Phosphorus
       </i>
       the morning star.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BBC-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">
        Pythagoras
       </a>
       is credited with realizing they were the same planet. There is no evidence that any of these cultures knew of the transits. Venus was important to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_pre-Columbian_civilizations" title="List of pre-Columbian civilizations">
        ancient American civilizations
       </a>
       , in particular for the
       <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">
        Maya
       </a>
       , who called it
       <i>
        Noh Ek
       </i>
       , "the Great Star" or
       <i>
        Xux Ek
       </i>
       , "the Wasp Star";
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       they embodied Venus in the form of the god
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kukulk%C3%A1n" title="Kukulkán">
        Kukulkán
       </a>
       (also known as or related to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gukumatz" title="Gukumatz">
        Gukumatz
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Quetzalcoatl" title="Quetzalcoatl">
        Quetzalcoatl
       </a>
       in other parts of Mexico). In the
       <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Codex" title="Dresden Codex">
        Dresden Codex
       </a>
       , the Maya charted Venus's full cycle, but despite their precise knowledge of its course, there is no mention of a transit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dresden_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dresden-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, it has been proposed that frescoes found at
       <a href="/wiki/Mayapan" title="Mayapan">
        Mayapan
       </a>
       may contain a pictorial representation of the 12th or 13th century transits.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Persian polymath
       <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">
        Avicenna
       </a>
       claimed to have observed Venus as a spot on the Sun. This is possible, as there was a transit on May 24, 1032, but Avicenna did not give the date of his observation, and modern scholars have questioned whether he could have observed the transit from his location at that time; he may have mistaken a
       <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">
        sunspot
       </a>
       for Venus. He used his transit observation to help establish that Venus was, at least sometimes, below the Sun in Ptolemaic cosmology,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ragep_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ragep-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       i.e. the sphere of Venus comes before the sphere of the Sun when moving out from the Earth in the prevailing
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geocentric" title="Geocentric">
        geocentric
       </a>
       model.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldstein_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Goldstein-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="1639_.E2.80.93_first_scientific_observation">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="1639_–_first_scientific_observation">
        1639 – first scientific observation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Transit_of_Venus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1639 – first scientific observation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JeremiahHorrocks.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="284" data-file-width="400" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/JeremiahHorrocks.jpg/220px-JeremiahHorrocks.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/JeremiahHorrocks.jpg/330px-JeremiahHorrocks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cf/JeremiahHorrocks.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JeremiahHorrocks.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_Horrocks" title="Jeremiah Horrocks">
          Jeremiah Horrocks
         </a>
         makes the first observation of the transit of Venus in 1639, as imagined by the artist
         <a href="/wiki/William_Richard_Lavender" title="William Richard Lavender">
          W. R. Lavender
         </a>
         in 1903
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_1639" title="Transit of Venus, 1639">
        Transit of Venus, 1639
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1627,
       <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">
        Johannes Kepler
       </a>
       became the first person to predict a transit of Venus, by predicting the 1631 event. His methods were not sufficiently accurate to predict that the transit would not be visible in most of Europe, and as a consequence, nobody was able to use his prediction to observe the phenomenon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The first recorded observation of a transit of Venus was made by
       <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_Horrocks" title="Jeremiah Horrocks">
        Jeremiah Horrocks
       </a>
       from his home at
       <a href="/wiki/Carr_House" title="Carr House">
        Carr House
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Much_Hoole" title="Much Hoole">
        Much Hoole
       </a>
       , near
       <a href="/wiki/Preston,_Lancashire" title="Preston, Lancashire">
        Preston
       </a>
       in England, on 4 December 1639 (24 November under the
       <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">
        Julian calendar
       </a>
       then in use in England). His friend,
       <a href="/wiki/William_Crabtree" title="William Crabtree">
        William Crabtree
       </a>
       , also observed this transit from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broughton,_Greater_Manchester" title="Broughton, Greater Manchester">
        Broughton
       </a>
       , near
       <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">
        Manchester
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kollerstrom_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kollerstrom-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Kepler had predicted transits in 1631 and 1761 and a near miss in 1639. Horrocks corrected Kepler's calculation for the orbit of Venus, realized that transits of Venus would occur in pairs 8 years apart, and so predicted the transit of 1639.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCL-book_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-UCL-book-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although he was uncertain of the exact time, he calculated that the transit was to begin at approximately 15:00. Horrocks focused the image of the Sun through a simple
       <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">
        telescope
       </a>
       onto a piece of paper, where the image could be safely observed. After observing for most of the day, he was lucky to see the transit as clouds obscuring the Sun cleared at about 15:15, just half an hour before sunset. Horrocks's observations allowed him to make a well-informed guess as to the size of Venus, as well as to make an estimate of the mean distance between the Earth and the Sun — the
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">
        astronomical unit
       </a>
       . He estimated that distance to be 59.4 million miles (95.6
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigametre" title="Gigametre">
        Gm
       </a>
       , 0.639
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">
        AU
       </a>
       ) – about two thirds of the actual distance of 93 million miles (149.6 million km), but a more accurate figure than any suggested up to that time. The observations were not published until 1661, well after Horrocks's death.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCL-book_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-UCL-book-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="1761_and_1769">
        1761 and 1769
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Transit_of_Venus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1761 and 1769">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Halley_1716_proposal_of_determining_the_parallax_of_the_sun.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="337" data-file-width="600" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Halley_1716_proposal_of_determining_the_parallax_of_the_sun.jpg/220px-Halley_1716_proposal_of_determining_the_parallax_of_the_sun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Halley_1716_proposal_of_determining_the_parallax_of_the_sun.jpg/330px-Halley_1716_proposal_of_determining_the_parallax_of_the_sun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Halley_1716_proposal_of_determining_the_parallax_of_the_sun.jpg/440px-Halley_1716_proposal_of_determining_the_parallax_of_the_sun.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Halley_1716_proposal_of_determining_the_parallax_of_the_sun.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Diagram from
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edmund_Halley" title="Edmund Halley">
          Edmund Halley
         </a>
         's 1716 paper to the Royal Society showing how the Venus transit could be used to calculate the distance between the Earth and the Sun
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_Transit_%26_Parallax.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="950" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Venus_Transit_%26_Parallax.svg/220px-Venus_Transit_%26_Parallax.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Venus_Transit_%26_Parallax.svg/330px-Venus_Transit_%26_Parallax.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Venus_Transit_%26_Parallax.svg/440px-Venus_Transit_%26_Parallax.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_Transit_%26_Parallax.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Measuring Venus transit times to determine
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_parallax" title="Solar parallax">
          solar parallax
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TransitOfVenus1769.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="936" data-file-width="740" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/TransitOfVenus1769.png/220px-TransitOfVenus1769.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/TransitOfVenus1769.png/330px-TransitOfVenus1769.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/TransitOfVenus1769.png/440px-TransitOfVenus1769.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TransitOfVenus1769.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Account of George III's observations of the 1769 transit.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/1769_Transit_of_Venus_observed_from_Tahiti" title="1769 Transit of Venus observed from Tahiti">
        1769 Transit of Venus observed from Tahiti
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1663 Scottish mathematician
       <a href="/wiki/James_Gregory_(mathematician)" title="James Gregory (mathematician)">
        James Gregory
       </a>
       had suggested in his
       <i>
        Optica Promota
       </i>
       that observations of a
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Mercury" title="Transit of Mercury">
        transit of the planet Mercury
       </a>
       , at widely spaced points on the surface of the Earth, could be used to calculate the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_parallax" title="Solar parallax">
        solar parallax
       </a>
       and hence the astronomical unit using
       <a href="/wiki/Triangulation" title="Triangulation">
        triangulation
       </a>
       . Aware of this, a young
       <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">
        Edmond Halley
       </a>
       made observations of such a transit on 28 October
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_style" title="Old style">
        O.S.
       </a>
       1677 from
       <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">
        Saint Helena
       </a>
       but was disappointed to find that only
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Towneley" title="Richard Towneley">
        Richard Towneley
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Burnley" title="Burnley">
        Burnley, Lancashire
       </a>
       had made another accurate observation of the event whilst Gallet, at Avignon, simply recorded that it had occurred. Halley was not satisfied that the resulting calculation of the solar parallax at 45" was accurate.
      </p>
      <p>
       In a paper published in 1691, and a more refined one in 1716, he proposed that more accurate calculations could be made using measurements of a transit of Venus, although the next such event was not due until 1761.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halley_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-halley-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Halley died in 1742, but in 1761 numerous expeditions were made to various parts of the world so that precise observations of the transit could be made in order to make the calculations as described by Halley—an early example of international scientific collaboration.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This collaboration was, however, underpinned by competition, the British, for example, being spurred to action only after they heard of French plans from
       <a href="/wiki/Joseph-Nicolas_Delisle" title="Joseph-Nicolas Delisle">
        Joseph-Nicolas Delisle
       </a>
       . In an attempt to observe the first transit of the pair, astronomers from Britain, Austria and France traveled to destinations around the world, including Siberia, Newfoundland and Madagascar.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RP_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-RP-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most managed to observe at least part of the transit, but successful observations were made in particular by
       <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_Dixon" title="Jeremiah Dixon">
        Jeremiah Dixon
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mason" title="Charles Mason">
        Charles Mason
       </a>
       at the
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">
        Cape of Good Hope
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MD_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MD-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Less successful, at
       <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">
        Saint Helena
       </a>
       , were
       <a href="/wiki/Nevil_Maskelyne" title="Nevil Maskelyne">
        Nevil Maskelyne
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Waddington_(mathematician)" title="Robert Waddington (mathematician)">
        Robert Waddington
       </a>
       , although they put the voyage to good use by trialling the
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_distance_(navigation)" title="Lunar distance (navigation)">
        lunar-distance method
       </a>
       of finding longitude.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lomonosov_1761German_Fig.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1026" data-file-width="770" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Lomonosov_1761German_Fig.jpg/220px-Lomonosov_1761German_Fig.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Lomonosov_1761German_Fig.jpg/330px-Lomonosov_1761German_Fig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Lomonosov_1761German_Fig.jpg/440px-Lomonosov_1761German_Fig.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lomonosov_1761German_Fig.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Diagrams from Mikhail Lomonosov's "The Appearance of Venus on the Sun, Observed at the St. Petersburg Imperial Academy of Sciences On 26 May 1761"
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rittenhouse_1769_observation_of_Venus_transit.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="383" data-file-width="600" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Rittenhouse_1769_observation_of_Venus_transit.jpg/220px-Rittenhouse_1769_observation_of_Venus_transit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Rittenhouse_1769_observation_of_Venus_transit.jpg/330px-Rittenhouse_1769_observation_of_Venus_transit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Rittenhouse_1769_observation_of_Venus_transit.jpg/440px-Rittenhouse_1769_observation_of_Venus_transit.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rittenhouse_1769_observation_of_Venus_transit.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Diagram from
         <a href="/wiki/David_Rittenhouse" title="David Rittenhouse">
          David Rittenhouse
         </a>
         's observations of the 1769 transit of Venus
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The existence of an
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">
        atmosphere on Venus
       </a>
       was concluded by
       <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lomonosov" title="Mikhail Lomonosov">
        Mikhail Lomonosov
       </a>
       on the basis of his observation of the transit of Venus of 1761 from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Academy_of_Sciences_of_St._Petersburg" title="Imperial Academy of Sciences of St. Petersburg">
        Imperial Academy of Sciences of St. Petersburg
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He used a two-lens achromat refractor and a weak solar filter (smoked glass) and reported seeing a bump or bulge of light ("Lomonosov's arc") off the solar disc as Venus began to exit the Sun. Lomonosov attributed that effect to refraction of solar rays through an atmosphere; he also reported the appearance of a sliver around the part of Venus that had just entered the Sun's disk during the initial phase of transit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ML_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ML-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2012, Pasachoff and Sheehan
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       reported, based on knowing what Venus's atmosphere would look like because of Pasachoff and Schneider's observations of the 2004 transit of Venus, that what Lomonosov reported was not Venus's atmosphere. To make a decisive test, a group of researchers carried out experimental reconstruction of Lomonosov's discovery of Venusian atmosphere with antique refractors during the transit of Venus on 5–6 June 2012.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They observed the "Lomonosov's arc" and other aureole effects due to Venus's atmosphere and concluded that Lomonosov's telescope was fully adequate to the task of detecting the arc of light around Venus off the Sun's disc during ingress or egress if proper experimental techniques as described by Lomonosov in his 1761 paper are employed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       For the 1769 transit, scientists traveled to
       <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">
        Tahiti
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Norway, and locations in North America including Canada, New England, and
       <a href="/wiki/San_Jos%C3%A9_del_Cabo" title="San José del Cabo">
        San José del Cabo
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Baja_California" title="Baja California">
        Baja California
       </a>
       , then under Spanish control). The Czech astronomer
       <a href="/wiki/Christian_Mayer_(astronomer)" title="Christian Mayer (astronomer)">
        Christian Mayer
       </a>
       was invited by
       <a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">
        Catherine the Great
       </a>
       to observe the transit in
       <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">
        Saint Petersburg
       </a>
       with
       <a href="/wiki/Anders_Johan_Lexell" title="Anders Johan Lexell">
        Anders Johan Lexell
       </a>
       , while other members of the
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Russian Academy of Sciences">
        Russian Academy of Sciences
       </a>
       went to eight other locations in the
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">
        Russian Empire
       </a>
       , under the general coordination of
       <a href="/wiki/Stepan_Rumovsky" title="Stepan Rumovsky">
        Stepan Rumovsky
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">
        George III of the United Kingdom
       </a>
       had the
       <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Observatory" title="King's Observatory">
        King's Observatory
       </a>
       built near his summer residence at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richmond_Lodge" title="Richmond Lodge">
        Richmond Lodge
       </a>
       for him and his royal astronomer
       <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Demainbray" title="Stephen Demainbray">
        Stephen Demainbray
       </a>
       to observe the transit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Hungarian astronomer
       <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_Hell" title="Maximilian Hell">
        Maximilian Hell
       </a>
       and his assistant
       <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_Sajnovics" title="János Sajnovics">
        János Sajnovics
       </a>
       traveled to
       <a href="/wiki/Vard%C3%B8" title="Vardø">
        Vardø
       </a>
       , Norway, delegated by
       <a href="/wiki/Christian_VII_of_Denmark" title="Christian VII of Denmark">
        Christian VII of Denmark
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/William_Wales_(astronomer)" title="William Wales (astronomer)">
        William Wales
       </a>
       and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Dymond&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Joseph Dymond (page does not exist)">
        Joseph Dymond
       </a>
       made their observation in
       <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">
        Hudson Bay
       </a>
       , Canada, for the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">
        Royal Society
       </a>
       . Observations were made by a number of groups in the British colonies in America. In
       <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">
        Philadelphia
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">
        American Philosophical Society
       </a>
       erected three temporary observatories and appointed a committee, of which
       <a href="/wiki/David_Rittenhouse" title="David Rittenhouse">
        David Rittenhouse
       </a>
       was the head. Observations were made by a group led by Dr. Benjamin West in
       <a href="/wiki/Providence,_Rhode_Island" title="Providence, Rhode Island">
        Providence, Rhode Island
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and published in 1769.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The results of the various observations in the American colonies were printed in the first volume of the American Philosophical Society's
       <i>
        Transactions
       </i>
       , published in 1771.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Comparing the North American observations, William Smith published in 1771 a best value of the solar parallax of 8.48 to 8.49 arc-seconds,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which corresponds to an Earth-Sun distance of 24,000 times the Earth's radius, about 3% different from the correct value.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/1769_Transit_of_Venus_observed_from_Tahiti" title="1769 Transit of Venus observed from Tahiti">
        Observations were also made from Tahiti
       </a>
       by James Cook and Charles Green at a location still known as "Point Venus".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This occurred on the
       <a href="/wiki/First_voyage_of_James_Cook" title="First voyage of James Cook">
        first voyage of James Cook
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cook-book_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cook-book-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       after which Cook explored
       <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">
        New Zealand
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
        Australia
       </a>
       . This was one of five expeditions organised by the Royal Society and the
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomer_Royal" title="Astronomer Royal">
        Astronomer Royal
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Nevil_Maskelyne" title="Nevil Maskelyne">
        Nevil Maskelyne
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Chappe_d%27Auteroche" title="Jean-Baptiste Chappe d'Auteroche">
        Jean-Baptiste Chappe d'Auteroche
       </a>
       went to San José del Cabo in what was then
       <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">
        New Spain
       </a>
       to observe the transit with two Spanish astronomers (Vicente de Doz and Salvador de Medina). For his trouble he died in an epidemic of
       <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">
        yellow fever
       </a>
       there shortly after completing his observations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Only 9 of 28 in the entire party returned home alive.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_Drawing.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="899" data-file-width="734" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Venus_Drawing.jpg/170px-Venus_Drawing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Venus_Drawing.jpg/255px-Venus_Drawing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Venus_Drawing.jpg/340px-Venus_Drawing.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_Drawing.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The "
         <a href="/wiki/Black_drop_effect" title="Black drop effect">
          black drop effect
         </a>
         " as recorded during the 1769 transit
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1882_transit_of_venus.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1280" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/1882_transit_of_venus.jpg/220px-1882_transit_of_venus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/1882_transit_of_venus.jpg/330px-1882_transit_of_venus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/1882_transit_of_venus.jpg/440px-1882_transit_of_venus.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1882_transit_of_venus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The 1882
         <a href="/wiki/Transit_(astronomy)" title="Transit (astronomy)">
          transit
         </a>
         of Venus
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The unfortunate
       <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Le_Gentil" title="Guillaume Le Gentil">
        Guillaume Le Gentil
       </a>
       spent eight years travelling in an attempt to observe either of the transits. His unsuccessful journey led to him losing his wife and possessions and being declared dead (his efforts became the basis of the play
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus_(play)" title="Transit of Venus (play)">
         Transit of Venus
        </a>
       </i>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Maureen_Hunter" title="Maureen Hunter">
        Maureen Hunter
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RP_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-RP-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Under the influence of the Royal Society
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ru%C4%91er_Bo%C5%A1kovi%C4%87" title="Ruđer Bošković">
        Ruđer Bošković
       </a>
       travelled to
       <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">
        Istanbul
       </a>
       , but arrived too late.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Unfortunately, it was impossible to time the exact moment of the start and end of the transit because of the phenomenon known as the "
       <a href="/wiki/Black_drop_effect" title="Black drop effect">
        black drop effect
       </a>
       ". This effect was long thought to be due to Venus's thick atmosphere, and initially it was held to be the first real evidence that Venus had an atmosphere. However, recent studies demonstrate that it is an optical effect caused by the smearing of the image of Venus by turbulence in the Earth's atmosphere or imperfections in the viewing apparatus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aas_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-aas-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-economist-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1771, using the combined 1761 and 1769 transit data, the French astronomer
       <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Lalande" title="Jérôme Lalande">
        Jérôme Lalande
       </a>
       calculated the astronomical unit to have a value of 153 million kilometers (±1 million km). The precision was less than had been hoped for because of the black drop effect, but still a considerable improvement on Horrocks's calculations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RP_24-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-RP-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Maximilian Hell published the results of his expedition in 1770, in Copenhagen.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Based on the results of his own expedition, and of Wales and Cook, in 1772 he presented another calculation of the astronomical unit: 151.7 million kilometers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Lalande queried the accuracy and authenticity of the Hell expedition, but later he retreated in an article of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Journal_des_s%C3%A7avans" title="Journal des sçavans">
         Journal des sçavans
        </a>
       </i>
       , in 1778.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="1874_and_1882">
        1874 and 1882
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Transit_of_Venus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1874 and 1882">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_1874" title="Transit of Venus, 1874">
        Transit of Venus, 1874
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_1882" title="Transit of Venus, 1882">
        Transit of Venus, 1882
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Transit observations in 1874 and 1882 allowed this value to be refined further. Three expeditions—from Germany, the United Kingdom and the United States—were sent to the
       <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_Islands" title="Kerguelen Islands">
        Kerguelen Archipelago
       </a>
       for the 1874 observations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The American astronomer
       <a href="/wiki/Simon_Newcomb" title="Simon Newcomb">
        Simon Newcomb
       </a>
       combined the data from the last four transits, and he arrived at a value of about 149.59 million kilometers (±0.31 million
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilometer" title="Kilometer">
        kilometers
       </a>
       ). Modern techniques, such as the use of radio
       <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">
        telemetry
       </a>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">
        space probes
       </a>
       , and of
       <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">
        radar
       </a>
       measurements of the distances to planets and
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroids
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       , have allowed a reasonably accurate value for the
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">
        astronomical unit
       </a>
       (AU) to be calculated to a precision of about ±30 meters. As a result, the need for parallax calculations has been superseded.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RP_24-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-RP-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_47-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-economist-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="2004_and_2012">
        2004 and 2012
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Transit_of_Venus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2004 and 2012">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_2004" title="Transit of Venus, 2004">
        Transit of Venus, 2004
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus,_2012" title="Transit of Venus, 2012">
        Transit of Venus, 2012
       </a>
      </div>

       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20040608_Venus_Transit.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Transit of Venus from
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degania_A" title="Degania A">
          Degania A
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
          Israel
         </a>
         , 2004
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>

            Play media
           </span>
          </span>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Transit_of_Venus,_2012_Orbital_Paths.ogv" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         This visualization shows the orbital paths of Venus and
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_(planet)" title="Earth (planet)">
          Earth
         </a>
         that led to this rare alignment on 5–6 June 2012
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A number of scientific organizations headed by the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Southern_Observatory" title="European Southern Observatory">
        European Southern Observatory
       </a>
       (ESO) organized a network of amateur astronomers and students to measure Earth's distance from the Sun during the transit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The participants' observations allowed a calculation of the astronomical unit (AU) of 149,608,708 km ± 11,835 km which had only a 0.007% difference to the accepted value.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There was a good deal of interest in the 2004 transit as scientists attempted to measure the pattern of light dimming as Venus blocked out some of the Sun's light, in order to refine techniques that they hope to use in searching for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">
        extrasolar planets
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_47-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-economist-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NS_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NS-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Current methods of looking for planets orbiting other stars only work for a few cases: planets that are very large (
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       -like, not Earth-like), whose
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">
        gravity
       </a>
       is strong enough to wobble the star sufficiently for us to detect changes in
       <a href="/wiki/Proper_motion" title="Proper motion">
        proper motion
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" title="Doppler shift">
        Doppler shift
       </a>
       changes in
       <a href="/wiki/Radial_velocity" title="Radial velocity">
        radial velocity
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Hot_Jupiter" title="Hot Jupiter">
        Jupiter or Neptune sized planets
       </a>
       very close to their parent star whose transit causes changes in the luminosity of the star; or planets which pass in front of background stars with the planet-parent star separation comparable to the
       <a href="/wiki/Einstein_ring" title="Einstein ring">
        Einstein ring
       </a>
       and cause
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_microlensing" title="Gravitational microlensing">
        gravitational microlensing
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Measuring light intensity during the course of a transit, as the planet blocks out some of the light, is potentially much more sensitive, and might be used to find smaller planets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist_47-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-economist-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, extremely precise measurement is needed: for example, the transit of Venus causes the amount of light received from the Sun to drop by a fraction of 0.001 (that is, to 99.9% of its nominal value), and the dimming produced by small extrasolar planets will be similarly tiny.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The 2012 transit provided scientists numerous research opportunities as well, in particular in regard to the study of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exoplanets" title="Exoplanets">
        exoplanets
       </a>
       . Research of the 2012 Venus transit includes:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Measuring dips in a star's brightness caused by a known planet transiting the Sun will help astronomers find exoplanets. Unlike the 2004 Venus transit, the 2012 transit occurred during an active phase of the 11-year activity cycle of the Sun, and it is likely to give astronomers practice in picking up a planet's signal around a "spotty" variable star.
       </li>
       <li>
        Measurements made of the apparent diameter of Venus during the transit, and comparison with its known diameter, will give scientists an idea of how to estimate exoplanet sizes.
       </li>
       <li>
        Observation made of the atmosphere of Venus simultaneously from Earth-based telescopes and from the
        <a href="/wiki/Venus_Express" title="Venus Express">
         Venus Express
        </a>
        gives scientists a better opportunity to understand the intermediate level of Venus's atmosphere than is possible from either viewpoint alone. This will provide new information about the
        <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">
         climate
        </a>
        of the planet.
       </li>
       <li>
        Spectrographic data taken of the well-known atmosphere of Venus will be compared to studies of exoplanets whose atmospheres are thus far unknown.
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
         Hubble Space Telescope
        </a>
        , which cannot be pointed directly at the Sun, used the
        <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
         Moon
        </a>
        as a mirror to study the light that had passed through the atmosphere of Venus in order to determine its composition. This will help to show whether a similar technique could be used to study exoplanets.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Past_and_future_transits">
        Past and future transits
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Transit_of_Venus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Past and future transits">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       NASA maintains a catalog of Venus transits covering the period 2000 BCE to 4000 CE.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Currently, transits occur only in June or December (see table) and the occurrence of these events slowly drifts, becoming later in the year by about two days every 243-year cycle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Transits usually occur in pairs, on nearly the same date eight years apart. This is because the length of eight Earth years is almost the same as 13 years on Venus, so every eight years the planets are in roughly the same relative positions. This approximate conjunction usually results in a pair of transits, but it is not precise enough to produce a triplet, since Venus arrives 22 hours earlier each time. The last transit not to be part of a pair was in 1396. The next will be in 3089; in 2854 (the second of the 2846/2854 pair), although Venus will just miss the Sun as seen from the Earth's equator, a partial transit will be visible from some parts of the southern hemisphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nao1_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nao1-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Thus after 243 years the transits of Venus return. The 1874 transit is a member of the 243-year cycle #1. The 1882 transit is a member of #2. The 2004 transit is a member of #3 and the 2012 transit is a member of #4. The 2117 transit is a member of #1 and so on. However, the ascending node (December transits) of the orbit of Venus moves backwards after each 243 years so the transit of 2854 is the last member of series #3 instead of series #1. The descending node (June transits) moves forwards, so the transit of 3705 is the last member of #2. From −125,000 till +125,000 there are only about ten 243-year series at both nodes regarding all the transits of Venus in this very long time-span, because both nodes of the orbit of Venus move back and forward in time as seen from the Earth.
      </p>

         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_Hell" title="Maximilian Hell">
          Maximilian Hell
         </a>
         to
         <a href="/wiki/Vard%C3%B8" title="Vardø">

         </a>
         , Norway.
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.hmnao.com/nao/transit/V_1769/" rel="nofollow">

      <p>
       Over longer periods of time, new series of transits will start and old series will end. Unlike the
       <a href="/wiki/Saros_(astronomy)#Saros_series" title="Saros (astronomy)">
        saros series
       </a>
       for lunar eclipses, it is possible for a transit series to restart after a hiatus. The transit series also vary much more in length than the saros series.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grazing_and_simultaneous_transits">
        Grazing and simultaneous transits
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Transit_of_Venus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Grazing and simultaneous transits">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Sometimes Venus only grazes the Sun during a transit. In this case it is possible that in some areas of the Earth a full transit can be seen while in other regions there is only a partial transit (no
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_transit#Contacts" title="Astronomical transit">
        second or third contact
       </a>
       ). The last transit of this type was on 6 December 1631, and the next such transit will occur on 13 December 2611.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FENASA_9-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-FENASA-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is also possible that a transit of Venus can be seen in some parts of the world as a partial transit, while in others Venus misses the Sun. Such a transit last occurred on 19 November 541 BC, and the next transit of this type will occur on 14 December 2854.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FENASA_9-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-FENASA-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These effects occur due to
       <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">
        parallax
       </a>
       , since the size of the Earth affords different points of view with slightly different lines of sight to Venus and the Sun. It can be demonstrated by closing an eye and holding a finger in front of a smaller more distant object; when you open the other eye and close the first, the finger will no longer be in front of the object.
      </p>
      <p>
       The simultaneous occurrence of a
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Mercury" title="Transit of Mercury">
        transit of Mercury
       </a>
       and a transit of Venus does occur, but extremely infrequently. Such an event last occurred on 22 September
       <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">
        373,173 BC
       </a>
       and will next occur on 26 July
       <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_far_future#Astronomical_events" title="Timeline of the far future">
        69,163
       </a>
       , and again on 29 March
       <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_far_future#Astronomical_events" title="Timeline of the far future">
        224,508
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S&amp;TAug2004_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-S&amp;TAug2004-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FENASAM_65-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FENASAM-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The simultaneous occurrence of a
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">
        solar eclipse
       </a>
       and a transit of Venus is currently possible, but very rare. The next solar eclipse occurring during a transit of Venus will be on 5 April
       <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_far_future#Astronomical_events" title="Timeline of the far future">
        15,232
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S&amp;TAug2004_64-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-S&amp;TAug2004-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The last time a solar eclipse occurred during a transit of Venus was on 1 November 15,607 BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The day after the Venerean transit of 3 June 1769 there was a total solar eclipse,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messieretal1769_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Messieretal1769-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which was visible in
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_America" title="Northern America">
        Northern America
       </a>
       , Europe and Northern Asia.
      </p>

      <p>
       <b>
        General
       </b>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="https://archive.is/20121210025329/http://brightstartutors.com/blog/2012/04/26/the-transit-of-venus/" rel="nofollow">
         Venus Transits: Measuring the Solar System
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.transitofvenus.nl/history.html" rel="nofollow">
         Historical observations of the transit of Venus
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110624032215/http://eclipse.gsfc.nasa.gov/transit/HalleyParallax.html" rel="nofollow">
         Edmond Halley's 1716 paper
        </a>
        describing how transits could be used to measure the Sun's distance, translated from Latin.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.sil.si.edu/Exhibitions/chasing-venus/index.htm" rel="nofollow">
         Chasing Venus: Observing the Transits of Venus, 1631–2004 (Smithsonian Libraries)
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <cite class="citation web">
         Merrifield, Michael.
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.sixtysymbols.com/videos/venus_transit.htm" rel="nofollow">
          "Venus Transit"
         </a>
         .
         <i>
          Sixty Symbols
         </i>
         .
         <a href="/wiki/Brady_Haran" title="Brady Haran">
          Brady Haran
         </a>
         for the
         <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nottingham" title="University of Nottingham">
          University of Nottingham
         </a>
         .
        </cite>
        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Sixty+Symbols&amp;rft.atitle=Venus+Transit&amp;rft.aulast=Merrifield&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.sixtysymbols.com%2Fvideos%2Fvenus_transit.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATransit+of+Venus">
         <span style="display:none;">
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <b>
        June 2012 transit
       </b>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://web.williams.edu/astronomy/eclipse/transits/ToV2012/index.htm" rel="nofollow">
         2012 Transit of Venus – International Astronomical Union
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120518015045/http://venustransit.nso.edu/" rel="nofollow">
         National Solar Observatory – Transit of Venus 5–6 June 2012
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20111126172216/http://www.sems.und.edu/" rel="nofollow">
         2012 Transit of Venus Live Webcast and chatroom with SEMS at UND live From Alaska
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
